<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41586"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2011 480:7378"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2011-11-16"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2011 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Sequencing of Medicago truncatula, a model organism of legume biology, shows that genome duplications had a role in the evolution of endosymbiotic nitrogen fixation. Legumes are unusual among plants in that they can carry out endosymbiotic nitrogen fixation with rhizobial bacteria. The genome of Medicago truncatula (also known as barrel medic or barrel clover), a well-established model for the study of legume biology, has now been sequenced. Genome analysis shows that M. truncatula has undergone several rounds of whole-genome duplication, and that the duplication that took place approximately 58 million years ago played an important part in the evolution of endosymbiotic nitrogen fixation. Legumes (Fabaceae or Leguminosae) are unique among cultivated plants for their ability to carry out endosymbiotic nitrogen fixation with rhizobial bacteria, a process that takes place in a specialized structure known as the nodule. Legumes belong to one of the two main groups of eurosids, the Fabidae, which includes most species capable of endosymbiotic nitrogen fixation1. Legumes comprise several evolutionary lineages derived from a common ancestor 60 million years ago (Myr ago). Papilionoids are the largest clade, dating nearly to the origin of legumes and containing most cultivated species2. Medicago truncatula is a long-established model for the study of legume biology. Here we describe the draft sequence of the M. truncatula euchromatin based on a recently completed BAC assembly supplemented with Illumina shotgun sequence, together capturing &#8764;94% of all M. truncatula genes. A whole-genome duplication (WGD) approximately 58 Myr ago had a major role in shaping the M. truncatula genome and thereby contributed to the evolution of endosymbiotic nitrogen fixation. Subsequent to the WGD, the M. truncatula genome experienced higher levels of rearrangement than two other sequenced legumes, Glycine max and Lotus japonicus. M. truncatula is a close relative of alfalfa (Medicago sativa), a widely cultivated crop with limited genomics tools and complex autotetraploid genetics. As such, the M. truncatula genome sequence provides significant opportunities to expand alfalfa&#8217;s genomic toolbox."/>

                <section aria-labelledby="Abs3" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs3">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs3-content"><p>Legumes (Fabaceae or Leguminosae) are unique among cultivated plants for their ability to carry out endosymbiotic nitrogen fixation with rhizobial bacteria, a process that takes place in a specialized structure known as the nodule. Legumes belong to one of the two main groups of eurosids, the Fabidae, which includes most species capable of endosymbiotic nitrogen fixation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Wang, H. et al. Rosid radiation and the rapid rise of angiosperm-dominated forests. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 3853–3858 (2009)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2162">1</a></sup>. Legumes comprise several evolutionary lineages derived from a common ancestor 60 million years ago (Myr ago). Papilionoids are the largest clade, dating nearly to the origin of legumes and containing most cultivated species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Lavin, M., Herendeen, P. S. &amp; Wojciechowski, M. F. Evolutionary rates analysis of Leguminosae implicates a rapid diversification of lineages during the tertiary. Syst. Biol. 54, 575–594 (2005)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2166">2</a></sup>. <i>Medicago truncatula</i> is a long-established model for the study of legume biology. Here we describe the draft sequence of the <i>M. truncatula</i> euchromatin based on a recently completed BAC assembly supplemented with Illumina shotgun sequence, together capturing <span class="stix">∼</span>94% of all <i>M. truncatula</i> genes. A whole-genome duplication (WGD) approximately 58 Myr ago had a major role in shaping the <i>M. truncatula</i> genome and thereby contributed to the evolution of endosymbiotic nitrogen fixation. Subsequent to the WGD, the <i>M. truncatula</i> genome experienced higher levels of rearrangement than two other sequenced legumes, <i>Glycine max</i> and <i>Lotus japonicus</i>. <i>M. truncatula</i> is a close relative of alfalfa (<i>Medicago sativa</i>), a widely cultivated crop with limited genomics tools and complex autotetraploid genetics. As such, the <i>M. truncatula</i> genome sequence provides significant opportunities to expand alfalfa’s genomic toolbox.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Optical mapping indicates that the eight pseudomolecules of assembly Mt3.5 span a physical distance of 375 million base pairs (Mb), and fluorescence <i>in situ</i> hybridization indicates they extend from pericentromeres almost to telomeric ends (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Figs 1 and 2</a>). Altogether, Mt3.5 consists of 2,536 bacterial artificial chromosomes (BACs; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Tables 1 and 2</a>) with 273 physical gaps (including centromeres, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 3</a>) and 101 internal sequencing gaps. The pseudomolecules contain 246 Mb of non-redundant sequence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 2</a>) located entirely within the optical map (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). Another 146 unfinished BACs/BAC pools that cannot be placed on the optical map contribute 17.3 Mb. Regions not represented in pseudomolecules or unanchored BACs were captured through assembly of approximately 40× coverage Illumina sequencing, yielding 104.2 Mb of additional unique sequence. Although not directly tested, the Illumina sequence is expected to lie predominantly within the boundaries of pseudomolecules (see below). On the basis of expressed sequence tag alignments, the combined data sets capture <span class="stix">∼</span>94% of expressed genes, providing a highly informative platform for analysing the euchromatin of <i>M. truncatula</i>, although still at the draft stage.</p><p>Altogether there are 62,388 gene loci in Mt3.5 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>), with 14,322 gene predictions annotated as transposons. Pseudomolecules and unassigned BACs contain a total of 44,124 gene loci, 177,271 retroelement-related regions and 26,487 DNA transposons, and non-redundant Illumina assemblies contribute an additional 18,264 genes, 75,777 retrotransposon regions and 8,476 DNA transposons (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Tables 5–9</a>) along with 1,418 organellar insertions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Data 1</a>). For pseudomolecules and unassigned BACs, this translates to 16.8 genes, 67.6 retrotransposons and 10.1 DNA transposons per 100 kilobases (kb). Within Illumina sequence assemblies, gene density (17.1 per 100 kb) and retrotransposon density (72.2 per 100 kb) are similar to pseudomolecules and unassigned BACs, whereas DNA transposon density is lower (8.2 per 100 kb). Similarities in gene and transposon densities between BAC and Illumina sequences support the assertion that the Illumina sequence is euchromatic, although the possibility that some Illumina assemblies come from low-copy regions within heterochromatin can not be excluded. Considering only the 47,845 genes with experimental or database support (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 4</a>), the average <i>M. truncatula</i> gene is 2,211 bp in length, contains 4.0 exons, and has a coding sequence of 1,001 bp. These values are similar to those observed previously in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (2,174 bp), <i>Oryza sativa</i> (3,403 bp) and <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> (2,301 bp)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. I. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2287">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2290">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596–1604 (2006)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2293">6</a></sup>.</p><p>Recent analyses of plant genomes indicate a shared whole-genome hexaploidy (WGH) preceding the rosid–asterid split at 140–150 Myr ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Tang, H. et al. Unraveling ancient hexaploidy through multiply-aligned angiosperm gene maps. Genome Res. 18, 1944–1954 (2008)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2300">7</a></sup>. Duplication patterns and genomic comparisons strongly suggest an additional WGD approximately 58 Myr ago in the papilionoids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Pfeil, B. E., Schlueter, J. A., Shoemaker, R. C. &amp; Doyle, J. J. Placing paleopolyploidy in relation to taxon divergence: a phylogenetic analysis in legumes using 39 gene families. Syst. Biol. 54, 441–454 (2005)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2304">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cannon, S. B. et al. Polyploidy did not predate the evolution of nodulation in all legumes. PLoS ONE 5, e11630 (2010)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2307">9</a></sup>. Near the time of this WGD, papilionoids radiated into several clades, the largest of which split quickly into two subclades, the Hologalegina (including <i>M. truncatula</i> and <i>L. japonicus</i>) and the milletioids (including <i>G. max</i> and other phaseoloids) at about 54 Myr ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Lavin, M., Herendeen, P. S. &amp; Wojciechowski, M. F. Evolutionary rates analysis of Leguminosae implicates a rapid diversification of lineages during the tertiary. Syst. Biol. 54, 575–594 (2005)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2321">2</a></sup>. We therefore compared <i>M. truncatula</i> pseudomolecules with other sequenced plant genomes to learn more about shared synteny and genome duplication history.</p><p>There is significant macrosynteny among <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>L. japonicus</i> and <i>G. max</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 5a, b</a>). Conserved blocks, sometimes as large as chromosome arms, span most euchromatin in all three genomes. A given <i>M. truncatula</i> region is typically syntenic with one other <i>M. truncatula</i> region as a result of the approximately 58-Myr-ago WGD, usually in small blocks showing degraded synteny (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#Fig2">Fig. 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). A given <i>M. truncatula</i> region is most similar to two <i>G. max</i> regions via speciation at about 54 Myr ago and the <i>Glycine</i> WGD at &lt;13 Myr ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2369">10</a></sup> and less similar to two other <i>G. max</i> regions resulting from the <span class="stix">∼</span>58-Myr-ago and &lt;13-Myr-ago WGD events. A <i>M. truncatula</i> region is likewise most similar to one <i>L. japonicus</i> region via speciation at about 50 Myr ago and less similar to a second <i>L. japonicus</i> region as a result of the <span class="stix">∼</span>58-Myr-ago WGD. Finally, each <i>M. truncatula</i> region and its homeologue typically show similarity to three <i>Vitis vinifera</i> regions via the pre-rosid WGH. Exceptions to these patterns could be due to gene losses, gains, or rearrangements specific to the <i>M. truncatula</i> lineage, resulting in synteny being more evident between <i>M. truncatula</i> and other genomes than in self-comparisons. Indeed, self-comparisons within <i>M. truncatula</i> reveal few remnants of the legume-specific WGD (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#Fig2">Fig. 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). Whereas this seems paradoxical, it is probably explained by extensive gene fractionation between WGD-derived homeologues in <i>M. truncatula</i>. In <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>, two short regions on Mt1 and Mt3 resulting from the <span class="stix">∼</span>58-Myr-ago WGD are displayed beside microsyntenic regions of <i>G. max</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i>. As expected, many genes are microsyntenic between <i>M. truncatula</i> and <i>G. max</i> (ranging from 7/19 between Mt3 and Gm14 to 10/20 between Mt1 and Gm17). Between the two <i>M. truncatula</i> homeologues, however, only 6 out of 33 genes (or collapsed gene families) are microsyntenic, with a homeologue missing from one or the other duplicate (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 10</a>). Apparently, there have been many more changes, large and small, in <i>M. truncatula</i> than in <i>G. max</i> since the legume WGD. This is borne out by the fact that synteny blocks in <i>M. truncatula</i> are one-third the length of those remaining from the papilionoid WGD in <i>G. max</i> (524 kb against 1,503 kb) with the average number of homologous gene pairs per block correspondingly lower (12.4 against 31.0).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Circos diagram illustrating syntenic relationships between  Medicago, Glycine, Lotus  and  Vitis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: <b>Circos diagram illustrating syntenic relationships between </b> <b><i>Medicago</i></b>, <b><i>Glycine</i></b>, <b><i>Lotus</i></b> <b> and </b> <b><i>Vitis</i></b>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature10625/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10625/MediaObjects/41586_2011_Article_BFnature10625_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10625/MediaObjects/41586_2011_Article_BFnature10625_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="644"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Homologous gene pairs were identified for all pairwise comparisons between <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>G. max</i>, <i>L. japonicus</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> genomes. Syntenic regions associated with the ancestral WGD events were identified by visually inspection of corresponding dot-plots. The large Mt5–Mt8 synteny block (yellow) was found to have two syntenic regions in <i>L. japonicus</i> (red), four syntenic regions in <i>G. max</i> (blue) and three in <i>V. vinifera</i> (green).</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM13">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature10625/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="
                        Circos diagram illustrating the 
                        Medicago
                         WGD and selected gene families.
                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: 
                        <b>Circos diagram illustrating the </b>
                        <b><i>Medicago</i></b>
                        <b> WGD and selected gene families.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature10625/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10625/MediaObjects/41586_2011_Article_BFnature10625_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10625/MediaObjects/41586_2011_Article_BFnature10625_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="807"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The 963 WGD-derived paralogous gene pairs were examined for overlap with the nodule-enhanced gene list (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Data 2</a>). Resulting gene pairs were joined and plotted as either blue triangles (only one of the duplicates is nodule-enhanced) or red (both nodule enhanced). Gene densities of NBS-LRRs, NCRs and other defensin-like proteins are plotted against chromosome position. Density was calculated using a sliding window (100-kb window with 50-kb steps).</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM14">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature10625/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="
                        Microsynteny comparison between 
                        Medicago
                         homeologues and corresponding regions of 
                        Glycine
                         and 
                        Vitis.
                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: 
                        <b>Microsynteny comparison between </b>
                        <b><i>Medicago</i></b>
                        <b> homeologues and corresponding regions of </b>
                        <b><i>Glycine</i></b>
                        <b> and </b>
                        <b><i>Vitis.</i></b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature10625/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10625/MediaObjects/41586_2011_Article_BFnature10625_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10625/MediaObjects/41586_2011_Article_BFnature10625_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="301"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Microsyntenic genome segments are centred around Medtr3g104510/Medtr1g015890 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 10</a>), a duplicated region derived from the <span class="stix">∼</span>58-Myr-ago WGD event noted in orange. The &lt;13-Myr-ago <i>G. max</i>-specific WGD is coloured yellow. Orthologous/paralogous gene pairs are indicated through use of a common colour. White arrows represent genes with no syntenic homologue(s) in this genome region. Some of these genes may actually have a syntenic sequence in soybean but no corresponding model reported in the current annotation (<a href="http://www.phytozome.net/soybean">http://www.phytozome.net/soybean</a>).</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM15">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature10625/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The <i>M. truncatula</i> genome also has undergone high rates of local gene duplication. The ratio of related genes within local clusters compared to all genes in families is 0.339 in <i>M. truncatula</i>, 3.1-fold higher than in <i>G. max</i> and 1.6-fold higher than in <i>A. thaliana</i> or <i>P. trichocarpa</i>. (‘Local clusters’ are defined as genes in a family all within 100 gene models of one another.) The excess of local gene duplications in <i>M. truncatula</i> is observed genome-wide and affects many families. There are 2.63 times as many gene families with local duplications in <i>M. truncatula</i> compared with <i>G. max</i> (2,980 against 1,131), an excess that also is seen in detailed comparisons of syntenic regions in <i>M. truncatula</i> and <i>G. max</i>. We examined 16.3 Mb of Mt05 showing synteny to two large regions of Gm01 plus homeologous blocks on Gm02, Gm09 and Gm11. In these regions, 25.8% of <i>M. truncatula</i> genes are locally duplicated compared with just 8.0% in <i>G. max</i>. Local gene duplications and losses have contributed both to synteny disruptions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#Fig3">Fig. 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>) and to high gene count (62,388) in <i>M. truncatula</i>—a value nearly as high as the 65,781 total gene models in <i>G. max</i> despite its additional (&lt;13 Myr ago) WGD. Local gene duplications are evident in certain gene families, such as F-box genes, which have undergone pronounced expansions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 11</a>). <i>M. truncatula</i> also has experienced higher rates of base substitution compared to other plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). Assuming 58 Myr ago as the date of the legume WGD, then the rate of synonymous substitutions per site per year in <i>M. truncatula</i> is 1.08 × 10<sup>−8</sup>, 1.8 times faster than estimates in other vascular plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Lynch, M. &amp; Conery, J. S. The evolutionary fate and consequences of duplicate genes. Science 290, 1151–1155 (2000)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2682">11</a></sup>. Higher rates of mutation and greater levels of rearrangement in <i>M. truncatula</i> following the papilionoid duplication may have been driven by factors including short generation times, high selfing rates or small effective population sizes, although these characteristics are not unique to <i>M. truncatula</i>.</p><p>Legumes and actinorhizal species are capable of forming a specialized organ, the root nodule, a highly differentiated structure hosting nitrogen-fixing symbionts. Phylogenetic studies suggest that nodulation may have evolved multiple times in the Fabidae, but the observation that all nodulating species are contained within this single clade indicates that a predisposition to nodulate evolved in their common ancestor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Soltis, D. E. et al. Chloroplast gene sequence data suggest a single origin of the predisposition for symbiotic nitrogen fixation in angiosperms. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 92, 2647–2651 (1995)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2696">12</a></sup>. It is unknown whether nodulation with rhizobia preceded the divergence of the three legume subfamilies or evolved on multiple occassions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Doyle, J. J. &amp; Luckow, M. A. The rest of the iceberg. Legume diversity and evolution in a phylogenetic context. Plant Physiol. 131, 900–910 (2003)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2700">13</a></sup>. Nevertheless, rhizobial nodulation and the 58-Myr-ago WGD are features common to most papilionoid legumes and both occurred early in the emergence of the group<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Lavin, M., Herendeen, P. S. &amp; Wojciechowski, M. F. Evolutionary rates analysis of Leguminosae implicates a rapid diversification of lineages during the tertiary. Syst. Biol. 54, 575–594 (2005)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2704">2</a></sup>. Given that WGDs generate genetic redundancy that potentially facilitates the emergence of novel gene functions without compromising existing ones<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Freeling, M. &amp; Thomas, B. C. Gene-balanced duplications, like tetraploidy, provide predictable drive to increase morphological complexity. Genome Res. 16, 805–814 (2006)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2708">14</a></sup>, we examined the <i>M. truncatula</i> genome to ask whether the 58-Myr-ago WGD might have had a role in the evolution of rhizobial nodulation in <i>M. truncatula</i> and its relatives.</p><p>Nod factors are bacterial signalling molecules that initiate nodulation. Previous studies have shown that several of the plant components involved in the response to Nod factors also function in mycorrhizal signalling<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Oldroyd, G. E. &amp; Downie, J. A. Coordinating nodule morphogenesis with rhizobial infection in legumes. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 59, 519–546 (2008)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2722">15</a></sup>. However, some Nod factor receptors and transcription factors have distinctly nodulation-specific functions. Among these nodulation-specific components, we found that the Nod factor receptor, <i>NFP</i>, and the transcription factor, <i>ERN1</i>, each have paralogues, <i>LYR1</i> and <i>ERN2</i> respectively, that trace back to the papilionoid WGD based on genome location and synonymous substitution rate values (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Data 2</a>). Both sets of gene pairs also show contrasting expression patterns and functional specialization. <i>NFP</i> and <i>ERN1</i> are expressed predominantly in the nodule and are known to function in nodulation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Arrighi, J. F. et al. The Medicago truncatula lysine motif-receptor-like kinase gene family includes NFP and new nodule-expressed genes. Plant Physiol. 142, 265–279 (2006)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2751">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Middleton, P. H. et al. An ERF transcription factor in Medicago truncatula that is essential for Nod factor signal transduction. Plant Cell 19, 1221–1234 (2007)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2754">17</a></sup>, whereas <i>LYR1</i> and <i>ERN2</i> are highly expressed during mycorrhizal colonization (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). These observations indicate that two important nodulation-specific signalling components in <i>M. truncatula</i> might have evolved from more ancient genes originally functioning in mycorrhizal signalling and then duplicated by the 58-Myr-ago WGD. In the case of <i>M. truncatula</i> <i>NFP</i>/<i>LYR1</i>, this conclusion is supported by the observation that the apparent orthologue of <i>NFP</i> in the nodulating non-legume <i>Parasponia andersonii</i> functions in both nodule and mycorrhizal signalling<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Op den Camp, R. et al. LysM-type mycorrhizal receptor recruited for rhizobium symbiosis in nonlegume Parasponia. Science 331, 909–912 (2011)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2787">18</a></sup>. Thus, the 58-Myr-ago WGD seems to have led to sub-functionalization of an ancestral gene participating in both interactions, resulting in two homeologous genes that each performs just one of the original functions.</p><p>To assess further the contribution of the WGD to <i>M. truncatula</i> nodulation, we analysed expression of paralogous gene pairs using RNA-seq data from six different organs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Methods</a> 5.1). A total of 963 WGD-derived gene pairs were found (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Data 2</a>) with 618 pairs (1,046 genes) having RNA-seq data for one or both homeologue. We then determined the number of genes showing organ-enhanced expression (defined as genes with expression level in a single organ at least twice the level in any other) within the pseudomolecule and the WGD-derived gene sets (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 12</a>). In both cases, different organs contained markedly different numbers of genes with enhanced expression (<i>χ</i><sup>2</sup> with 5 degrees of freedom, <i>P</i> = 10<sup>−272</sup>); however, the rank order among the organs was identical. Roots had the largest number of genes with enhanced expression followed by flower, nodule, leaf, seed/pod and bud. Among gene pairs with nodule-enhanced expression, both paralogues were nodule-enhanced in eight pairs, whereas just a single paralogue was nodule-enhanced in the other 43 pairs. This is consistent with nodulation pre-dating the WGD and further sub- and neo-functionalization emerging afterwards. We went on to examine transcription factors because they can act as regulators of plant growth and development. A total of 3,692 putative TF genes were discovered (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Data 3</a>), representing 5.9% of all <i>M. truncatula</i> gene models (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 13</a>). Of the 1,513 TF genes on pseudomolecules with RNA-seq data, 142 genes (9.4%) derived from the 58-Myr-ago WGD (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Data 4</a>), consistent with previous observations indicating greater retention of transcription factors following polyploidy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Thomas, B. C., Pedersen, B. &amp; Freeling, M. Following tetraploidy in an Arabidopsis ancestor, genes were removed preferentially from one homeolog leaving clusters enriched in dose-sensitive genes. Genome Res. 16, 934–946 (2006)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2832">19</a></sup>. Nodule-enhanced expression was significantly higher among transcription factors (92 out of 1,513 or 6.1%) than among all pseudomolecule genes (1,111 out of 23,478 or 4.7%) (<i>χ</i><sup><i>2</i></sup> with 1 degree of freedom, <i>P</i> = 0.024) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 12</a>). Nodule-enhanced expression was even higher in WGD-derived transcription factors (11 out of 142 or 7.7%), although this enrichment did not reach statistical significance (<i>P</i> = 0.113). As expected, <i>ERN1</i> is found within this group of WGD-retained, nodule-enhanced transcription factors.</p><p>These results show that many paralogous genes retained from the 58-Myr-ago WGD, especially signalling components and regulators, have undergone sub- or neo-functionalization, including several with specialized roles in nodulation. Nevertheless, separate phylogenetic analyses (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Methods</a> 5.5) indicate that some nodule-related genes derive from the more ancient pre-rosid WGH, with their nodule-related functions pre-dating the 58-Myr-ago WGD (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Data 5</a>). Taken together, these results are consistent with a model where the capacity for primitive interaction with new symbionts derived from existing mycorrhizal machinery involving genes recruited from the pre-rosid WGH. This capacity would have arisen early in the Fabidae clade and led to the appearance of nodulation in multiple lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Doyle, J. J. &amp; Luckow, M. A. The rest of the iceberg. Legume diversity and evolution in a phylogenetic context. Plant Physiol. 131, 900–910 (2003)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2864">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Kistner, C. &amp; Parniske, M. Evolution of signal transduction in intracellular symbiosis. Trends Plant Sci. 7, 511–518 (2002)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2867">20</a></sup>. Later, the 58-Myr-ago WGD would have resulted in additional genes, including <i>NFP</i>, <i>ERN1</i> and the transcription factors described above, that went on to become specialized for nodule-related functions in the Papilionoideae.</p><p><i>Medicago</i> contains additional amplified gene families, many nodulation-related and found in tandem clusters. <i>M. truncatula</i> has nine symbiotic leghaemoglobins, more than twice the number in <i>L. japonicus</i> or <i>G. max</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). Five of these genes are located in a tight cluster on Mt5. The <i>M. truncatula</i> genome contains 593 nodule cysteine-rich peptides (NCRs) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Data 6</a>), a gene family restricted to <i>M. truncatula</i> and its relatives<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Kato, T. et al. Expression of genes encoding late nodulins characterized by a putative signal peptide and conserved cysteine residues is reduced in ineffective pea nodules. Mol. Plant Microbe Interact. 15, 129–137 (2002)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2904">21</a></sup>. NCRs are noteworthy because they include members essential for terminal differentiation of rhizobia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Van de Velde, W. et al. Plant peptides govern terminal differentiation of bacteria in symbiosis. Science 327, 1122–1126 (2010)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2908">22</a></sup>. NCRs are tightly clustered within the <i>M. truncatula</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>), with 75% found in clusters of up to 11 members. The <i>M. truncatula</i> genome also has 764 nucleotide-binding site and leucine-rich repeat (NBS-LRR) genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Data 7</a>), more than other plant genomes that have been sequenced so far<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Meyers, B. C., Kozik, A., Griego, A., Kuang, H. &amp; Michelmore, R. W. Genome-wide analysis of NBS-LRR-encoding genes in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 15, 809–834 (2003)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2925">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Yang, S., Zhang, X., Yue, J. X., Tian, D. &amp; Chen, J. Q. Recent duplications dominate NBS-encoding gene expansion in two woody species. Mol. Genet. Genomics 280, 187–198 (2008)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2928">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Zhou, T. et al. Genome-wide identification of NBS genes in japonica rice reveals significant expansion of divergent non-TIR NBS-LRR genes. Mol. Genet. Genomics 271, 402–415 (2004)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2931">25</a></sup>, many with nodule-specific expression (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>). Almost 90% of NBS-LRRs occur in clusters and genome regions showing limited macrosynteny to other species, such as Mt3 and Mt6, are locations of large NBS-LRR superclusters (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#Fig2">Fig. 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Tables 14 and 15</a>). Finally, <i>M. truncatula</i> secretes flavonoid signalling molecules to induce the <i>nod</i> genes of <i>Sinorhizobium meliloti</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Peters, N. K., Frost, J. W. &amp; Long, S. R. A plant flavone, luteolin, induces expression of Rhizobium meliloti nodulation genes. Science 233, 977–980 (1986)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2953">26</a></sup>. In <i>M. truncatula</i>, the corresponding biosynthetic pathway has expanded markedly, with 28 <i>M. truncatula</i> chalcone synthase genes in clusters of up to seven members compared to just four chalcone synthases in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Winkel-Shirley, B. Flavonoid biosynthesis. A colorful model for genetics, biochemistry, cell biology, and biotechnology. Plant Physiol. 126, 485–493 (2001)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2966">27</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Data 8</a>). <i>M. truncatula</i> has ten chalcone reductases compared to none in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Hegnauer, R. Relevance of seed polysaccharides and flavonoids for the classification of the leguminosae: a chemotaxonomic approach. Phytochemistry 34, 3–16 (1993)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2978">28</a></sup> and <i>M. truncatula</i> has 11 chalcone isomerase genes, including one cluster of seven members, compared to just one representative in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Shirley, B. W. et al. Analysis of Arabidopsis mutants deficient in flavonoid biosynthesis. Plant J. 8, 659–671 (1995)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d14237e2988">29</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Figs 15 and 16</a>).</p><p>Analysis of the <i>M. truncatula</i> genome supports earlier studies indicating that the dramatic radiation of the legume family (at least the papilionoid subfamily) is partly attributed to the 58-Myr-ago WGD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Singer, S. R. et al. Venturing beyond beans and peas: what can we learn from Chamaecrista? Plant Physiol. 151, 1041–1047 (2009)" href="/articles/nature10625#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d14237e3002">30</a></sup>. Our results indicate that the WGD early in papilionoid evolution allowed the emergence of critical components in Nod factor signalling and contributed to the complexity of rhizobial nodulation observed in this clade. As such, the WGD seems to have had a crucial role in the success of papilionoid legumes, enhancing their utility to humans.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods Summary"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods Summary</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">DNA sequencing</h3><p>Six A17 BAC and one fosmid library were used to create Mt3.5 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 1</a>). Most were processed by Sanger paired-end sequencing of 3–6-kb shotgun libraries. Sequences were downloaded in February/March 2009 with scaffolding performed by aligning all BAC and fosmid ends against contigs and then anchored and ordered primarily by optical mapping. Separately, 25 billion base pairs (Gb) of Illumina sequence was generated using short (375 nt) inserts plus 2.1 Gb from a 5 kb mate-pair library, then assembled using CLCbio (<a href="http://www.clcbio.com">http://www.clcbio.com</a>) and Soap (<a href="http://soap.genomics.org.cn/">http://soap.genomics.org.cn/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">RNA sequencing</h3><p>Five tissues were used for RNA-seq analysis with <span class="stix">∼</span>10 million Illumina 36-bp reads per library (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 12</a>). Three tissues were used for small RNA analysis with <span class="stix">∼</span>3 million reads per Illumina library (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Figs 17–18</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 16</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature10625#MOESM16">Supplementary Data 9</a>).</p></div></div></section>

                <p><i>Medicago truncatula</i> pseudomolecules are found at DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank as accession numbers <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=CM001217%96CM001224">CM001217–CM001224</a> and unanchored BACs as <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=GL982851%96GL982996">GL982851–GL982996</a>. Illumina genome sequences are in the Short Read Archive under <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRS150378">SRS150378</a>, RNA-seq sequences under <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRP008485">SRP008485</a>, and small RNA sequences in GEO under <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?acc=GSM769273">GSM769273</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?acc=GSM769274">GSM769274</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?acc=GSM769276">GSM769276</a>. Pseudomolecule annotation and Illumina assemblies are available at <a href="ftp://ftp.jcvi.org/pub/data/m_truncatula/Mt3.5/">ftp://ftp.jcvi.org/pub/data/m_truncatula/Mt3.5/</a>.</p>
              
            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Bib1" data-title="References"><div class="c-article-section" id="Bib1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Bib1">References</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Bib1-content"><div data-container-section="references"><ol class="c-article-references"><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="author" content="H. Wang, " /><meta itemprop="datePublished" content="2009" /><meta itemprop="headline" content="Wang, H. et al. Rosid radiation and the rapid rise of angiosperm-dominated forests. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci.                 <div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM20"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10625/MediaObjects/41586_2011_BFnature10625_MOESM20_ESM.xls" data-supp-info-image="">Supplementary Data 4</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>The data shows duplicate TF gene pairs predicted to be derived from the ~58 Mya WGD, including RNA-seq expression data. (XLS 68 kb)</p></div></div>
                  
                <div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM21"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10625/MediaObjects/41586_2011_BFnature10625_MOESM21_ESM.tar" data-supp-info-image="">Supplementary Data 5</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>The data shows graphic (.pdf) and .ph guide tree files for all 37 gene families used in the analysis of WGT and WGD events. (TAR 197 kb)</p></div></div>
                  
                <div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM22"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10625/MediaObjects/41586_2011_BFnature10625_MOESM22_ESM.xls" data-supp-info-image="">Supplementary Data 6</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>The data shows nodule cysteine-rich proteins in <i>Mt</i>3.5 pseudomolecules, BACs and Illumina contigs, including family assignment and evidence. (XLS 214 kb)</p></div></div>
                  
                <div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM23"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10625/MediaObjects/41586_2011_BFnature10625_MOESM23_ESM.xls" data-supp-info-image="">Supplementary Data 7</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><i>Mt</i>3.5 pseudomolecules, BACs and Illumina contigs, domain structure, expression, and heatmap data. (XLS 471 kb)</p></div></div>
                  
                <div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM24"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10625/MediaObjects/41586_2011_BFnature10625_MOESM24_ESM.xls" data-supp-info-image="">Supplementary Data 8</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>The data shows <i>Mt</i> genes related to chalcone, P450 or saponin metabolism in pseudomolecules, BACs and Illumina contigs. (XLS 3075 kb)</p></div></div>
                  
                <div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM25"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10625/MediaObjects/41586_2011_BFnature10625_MOESM25_ESM.pdf" data-supp-info-image="">Supplementary Data 9</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>The data shows <i>Mt</i> small RNAs with &gt;85% similarities to annotated At miRNAs in miRBase v15. (PDF 149 kb)</p></div></div></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="PowerPoint slides"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">PowerPoint slides</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info">
                  
                  
                  
                <div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM13"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10625/MediaObjects/41586_2011_BFnature10625_MOESM13_ESM.ppt" data-supp-info-image="">PowerPoint slide for Fig. 1</a></h3></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM14"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10625/MediaObjects/41586_2011_BFnature10625_MOESM14_ESM.ppt" data-supp-info-image="">PowerPoint slide for Fig. 2</a></h3></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM15"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature10625/MediaObjects/41586_2011_BFnature10625_MOESM15_ESM.ppt" data-supp-info-image="">PowerPoint slide for Fig. 3</a></h3></div></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="rightslink" data-title="Rights and permissions"><div class="c-article-section" id="rightslink-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="rightslink">Rights and permissions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="rightslink-content">

            <aside><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__container" data-component-show-more="true" id="editorial-summary"><section><h1 class="c-article-editorial-summary__title u-h3">Editorial Summary</h1><h2 class="c-article-editorial-summary__article-title">Evolution of nitrogen fixation in plants</h2><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__content c-article-editorial-summary__content--less"><p>Legumes are unusual among plants in that they can carry out endosymbiotic nitrogen fixation with rhizobial bacteria. The genome of <i>Medicago truncatula</i> (also known as barrel medic or barrel clover), a well-established model for the study of legume biology, has now been sequenced. Genome analysis shows that <i>M. truncatula</i> has undergone several rounds of whole-genome duplication, and that the duplication that took place approximately 58 million years ago played an important part in the evolution of endosymbiotic nitrogen fixation.</p></div></section></div></aside>
